<div id="review_270391103" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/270391103" />
    <a title="Marian Allen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3314240-marian-allen"><img alt="Marian Allen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442500425p2/3314240.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/270391103?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marian Allen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marian Allen" href="/user/show/3314240-marian-allen">Marian Allen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer270391103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9541720546043826376">Every author has the right to produce the book he or she has inside. It doesn't have to meet reader expectations or desires. What disappoints one reader will delight another. The only responsibility a writer has is to the work.<br/><br/>Other Terry Pratchett fans have told me that SNUFF is wonderful; they loved it.<br/><br/>I can't argue with that. All I can say is, if the rating is based on whether or not I, me, myself liked the book, the answer is no. I didn't.<br/><br/>Is Pratchett's signature sideways humor almost totall</span>
  <span id="freeText9541720546043826376" style="display:none">Every author has the right to produce the book he or she has inside. It doesn't have to meet reader expectations or desires. What disappoints one reader will delight another. The only responsibility a writer has is to the work.<br /><br />Other Terry Pratchett fans have told me that SNUFF is wonderful; they loved it.<br /><br />I can't argue with that. All I can say is, if the rating is based on whether or not I, me, myself liked the book, the answer is no. I didn't.<br /><br />Is Pratchett's signature sideways humor almost totally missing from this book, or am I so used to it, it doesn't strike me sideways anymore? Is he writing (perhaps understandably) darker these days?<br /><br />I used to read Pratchett for escape. SNUFF is dark enough to be a news broadcast.<br /><br />Mr. Pratchett doesn't owe me a vacation. I'm not claiming that he does. This book might be your cup of tea. It wasn't mine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9541720546043826376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_223238430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/223238430" />
    <a title="Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1605591-christine"><img alt="Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252166169p2/1605591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/223238430?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine" href="/user/show/1605591-christine">Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=sir-terry-pratchett">sir-terry-pratchett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=fantasy-humor">fantasy-humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223238430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5280079463230467059">Terry Pratchett is, perhaps, the world's best humanist. Terry Pratchett should also win the Nobel and Booker prizes, but odds are, he never will.<br/><br/>But at least, the Queen as knighted him.<br/><br/><i>Snuff</i> is Pratchett's latest novel, an installment in the Discworld, and features Sam Vimes, Pratchett's everyman copper hero and one of his alter-egos. Forced to go on vaccation at his wife's estate, Sam discovers there has been a murder.<br/><br/>And any follower of the Discworld knows what that means.<br/><br/><i>Snuff</i> is more in the </span>
  <span id="freeText5280079463230467059" style="display:none">Terry Pratchett is, perhaps, the world's best humanist. Terry Pratchett should also win the Nobel and Booker prizes, but odds are, he never will.<br /><br />But at least, the Queen as knighted him.<br /><br /><i>Snuff</i> is Pratchett's latest novel, an installment in the Discworld, and features Sam Vimes, Pratchett's everyman copper hero and one of his alter-egos. Forced to go on vaccation at his wife's estate, Sam discovers there has been a murder.<br /><br />And any follower of the Discworld knows what that means.<br /><br /><i>Snuff</i> is more in the tradition of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34484.Small_Gods__Discworld___13_" title="Small Gods (Discworld, #13) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Small Gods</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47989.Night_Watch__Discworld___29_" title="Night Watch (Discworld, #29) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Watch</a>. In other words, it is a more serious Discworld novel with the message heavily at the forefront. It is also a darker Discworld novel and far better than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34484.Small_Gods__Discworld___13_" title="Small Gods (Discworld, #13) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Small Gods</a>. <br /><br />Like all Discworld works, Pratchett refers to other standards of literautre - (Mr Pratchett, Sir Terry, I'm really sorry, but I do think some of what you write is literature) - in particular Jane Austen's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1885.Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice by Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Pride and Prejudice</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Mansfield Park by Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Mansfield Park</a>, but being Pratchett he puts his own twists on things.<br /><br />Oh, and besides the murder, Vimes (calling him Sam just feels wrong) has to deal with son, Young Sam, who is in the poo stage of life - and he really wants elephant poo because it goes dung.<br /><br />Pratchett's genius, his absolutely bloody genius, is his ability to deal not only in the commonplace but to make it's absurdities magical and to do so in such a way that as you are cracking up you are nodding your head.<br /><br />In many ways, Pratchett's characters are the most refreshing normal characters you will ever meet, even if they include a werewolf and a hero who thinks he is a dwarf. They are human, with the possible exceptation of Nobby. Vimes, for instance, is a carinig husband and father, and it is so nice to see a police officer or any central character for that matter, have one. Sybil has a large support role, and it is always nice to see Sybil and Sam work together.<br /><br />It's true that the pacing is a little slow in the beginning and towards the end. Yet in some ways, the story seems to call for that (like say the ending of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18512.The_Return_of_the_King__The_Lord_of_the_Rings___3_" title="The Return of the King (The Lord of the Rings, #3) by J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Return of the King</a>).<br /><br />Let me say, in this pre-presidential year, Ventarai for President. VP could be Vimes or Sybil. Wilkins could be head of the CIA, FBI, and Secret Service. He is so efficient.<br /><br />This book is a fine journey, a look at humanity with a well loved author and the friends that he has gifted the world with. If you haven't visited any of Pratchett's books, go and visit them now.<br /><br />That's a command.<br /><br />Mr, Sir, Pratchett, I'm not on the Booker or Nobel comittees. I don't have enough money to start a real prize. I, however, hereby award you the Lifetime Achivement Award for Writing also known as The Golden Banana Daiquiri.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5280079463230467059" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer989791207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11004635387257171532">There are so many levels upon which to enjoy a Pratchett book. There is the everyday functioning of a marriage; the rearing of a child, and the uneasy balance between encouragement and disgust that sometimes entails, as when young Sam becomes engrossed in the study of poo of many animals. Here we also have the tiniest of crimes and the greatest, although interestingly, the greatest is an individual murder, not genocide of a species. One thing I particularly liked: Vimes never loses track of just</span>
  <span id="freeText11004635387257171532" style="display:none">There are so many levels upon which to enjoy a Pratchett book. There is the everyday functioning of a marriage; the rearing of a child, and the uneasy balance between encouragement and disgust that sometimes entails, as when young Sam becomes engrossed in the study of poo of many animals. Here we also have the tiniest of crimes and the greatest, although interestingly, the greatest is an individual murder, not genocide of a species. One thing I particularly liked: Vimes never loses track of just how culpable the many players are, most especially not the bystanders who turned away.<br /><br />Pratchett never stops questioning what makes a human. I love that.<br /><br />Library copy</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12657371-snuff">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1994263142" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5367573335962393182"><i>Snuff</i> was the 8th and final novel in the <i>Watch</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. Vimes reluctantly goes off on a “vacation” to the country with his family. His impressions of the country, having spent all his life in the city of Ankh-Morpork, are amusing, and naturally he manages to find a mystery to solve which leads to very little relaxation.<br/><br/>I enjoyed the book, but I had hoped it might be more of an ensemble story. This was mostly a Vimes story and there was very, very little page time given to the othe</span>
  <span id="freeText5367573335962393182" style="display:none"><i>Snuff</i> was the 8th and final novel in the <i>Watch</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. Vimes reluctantly goes off on a “vacation” to the country with his family. His impressions of the country, having spent all his life in the city of Ankh-Morpork, are amusing, and naturally he manages to find a mystery to solve which leads to very little relaxation.<br /><br />I enjoyed the book, but I had hoped it might be more of an ensemble story. This was mostly a Vimes story and there was very, very little page time given to the other members of the Watch or any of the other Ankh-Morpork characters. It was still entertaining though, and it made a couple of boring flights seem to pass more quickly.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5367573335962393182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_162598399" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162598399" />
    <a title="Erastes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/151619-erastes"><img alt="Erastes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442472069p2/151619.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/162598399?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erastes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erastes" href="/user/show/151619-erastes">Erastes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer162598399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16628649535269023179">about ¾ of the way through and enjoying myself immensely. You'd have to be a real Sam Vimes fan I think though to truly "get" the book and to enjoy it as much as I am, it's classic Vimes and it shines a light on him away from Ankh and his job there. I know he's been away before, to Uberwald--but he was on official business at the time and here he's acting under his own steam even though he's justifying it! :D<br/><br/>Willikins is brilliant, as is Sybil as ever and Young Sam is exactly how he should be--t</span>
  <span id="freeText16628649535269023179" style="display:none">about ¾ of the way through and enjoying myself immensely. You'd have to be a real Sam Vimes fan I think though to truly "get" the book and to enjoy it as much as I am, it's classic Vimes and it shines a light on him away from Ankh and his job there. I know he's been away before, to Uberwald--but he was on official business at the time and here he's acting under his own steam even though he's justifying it! :D<br /><br />Willikins is brilliant, as is Sybil as ever and Young Sam is exactly how he should be--that one has a career as Ankh-Morpork's Sherlock in a few years - he's a copper to his boots and has a much more inquiring mind. Vimes wants to know whodunnit, but Sam wants to know - yes, and what was the rope made of that helped dunnit? More when I've finished!<br />==================<br />Ok - I've finished, and I've knocked a star off, because...<br /><br />Well, that was… interesting.<br /><br />90% of the book was wonderful. There was so much to love: Sam, of course, not realising until right at the end that he’d been micro managed—AS USUAL, by Vetinari with a big dollop of help from Sybil, Sam’s darkness and his real fear that it might take over, The Summoning Dark and the way it may—or may not—be living with Sam. Young Sam is just glorious and he made me tear up once or twice, he’s a copper (or will possibly be, as essayel pointed out) Ankh’s Sherlock in years to come) to his boots. He’s going to have an Interesting Life, that’s for sure. Lesbian couples, “the poo lady,” marvellous snobby country people and honest country folk… so much to love.<br /><br />But…<br /><br />I felt that part of the book was simply MISSING. There is so much that is introduced and then left up in the air that all the way through I was expecting an explanation, and simply never got it. This isn’t like Pratchett, he doesn’t always expound everything about a people—we’ve learned much about trolls and dwarves and other races over the course of the books, but generally we are told What We Need To Know At the Time. And I didn’t feel that we were told all we needed to know about the Goblins. I EXPECT a mystery and a half with Pratchett, things are usually vague in the beginning (like, for example in Hogfather where we are gradually given glimpses of events happening but much doesn’t make sense until after the Tooth Fairy's house is breached.) But there are things in Snuff that are simply never explained—other than “oh that’s Goblin Magic” or something.<br /><br />I didn’t understand the correlation between smuggling Troll Drugs and tobacco and Goblin pots – unless the pot was put into the tobacco as a desperate measure like a message in a bottle? But then if that was the case, how did they think that they’d be found?<br /><br />And I found the ending part to be—odd. Rushed, and that’s not something I’ve accused Pratchett of before. I didn’t really get what he and Sybil were doing, and why they had to do them seperately, and as for Vetinari’s last talk with Vimes, it was SO Out of Character it seemed to be written by someone else.<br /><br />I guess I’ll have to read it again, just in case i’m being incredibly dim.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16628649535269023179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_228020178" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228020178" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1177534-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593697601p2/1177534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228020178?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1177534-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228020178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5536403381814226641">With the news of Terry Pratchett's declining health, it feels as if each new Discworld novel could be his last. It also made you want to savor each entry a bit more. <br/><br/>Unfortunately, the last couple of entries haven't been quite among Pratchett's best. <br/><br/>Thankfully, <i>Snuff</i> is a return to form for Pratchett and while I hope we get more, if this is the last Discworld entry we get, it will be a solid, entertaining high note for the series to go out on. <br/><br/>As I've said in other Pratchett reviews, Pratchett </span>
  <span id="freeText5536403381814226641" style="display:none">With the news of Terry Pratchett's declining health, it feels as if each new Discworld novel could be his last. It also made you want to savor each entry a bit more. <br /><br />Unfortunately, the last couple of entries haven't been quite among Pratchett's best. <br /><br />Thankfully, <i>Snuff</i> is a return to form for Pratchett and while I hope we get more, if this is the last Discworld entry we get, it will be a solid, entertaining high note for the series to go out on. <br /><br />As I've said in other Pratchett reviews, Pratchett makes looking witty, funny and satirical look easy when he's on the top of his game. And he does that hear. The story of Sam Vines being forced to take a vacation to the countryside with his wife only to find a foul plot unfolding there is well done and keeps the story moving. Part of what makes it work is the constant running gag of how husbands have to defer to their wives on whether they enjoy consuming certain things like bacon, cigars and other products that may shorten the lifespan but are still eminently enjoyable. Add in a bit of social commentary and you've got the makings of one of the better Discworld novels in recent memory. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5536403381814226641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_663297613" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/663297613" />
    <a title="Kathleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen"><img alt="Kathleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365166675p2/13954899.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/663297613?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen" href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen">Kathleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/12889446-snuff">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=humorous">humorous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=mystery-crime-police">mystery-crime-police</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=magic-fantasy">magic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer663297613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7362309288673580598">3.5 stars. An enjoyable enough read, but not without it's problems: At times, it felt too pat. Vimes is too powerful, too famous, and just too glorious. It wasn't enough to be a Duke? And despite his street-gang portrayal in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62530.Thud___Discworld___34__City_Watch__7_' title='Thud! (Discworld, #34; City Watch #7) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Thud!</a>, Willikins is too dangerous, especially compared to his cherubic characterization in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400354.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch__2_' title='Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch #2) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Men at Arms</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47990.Jingo__Discworld___21__City_Watch__4_' title='Jingo (Discworld, #21; City Watch #4) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jingo</a>. The ending is too happy, wrapped up in a bow. Vimes and Sybil get naked a few times (never happened before, not even when begetting an heir) and the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1885.Pride_and_Prejudice' title='Pride and Prejudice by Jane Austen' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pride and Pr</a></span>
  <span id="freeText7362309288673580598" style="display:none">3.5 stars. An enjoyable enough read, but not without it's problems: At times, it felt too pat. Vimes is too powerful, too famous, and just too glorious. It wasn't enough to be a Duke? And despite his street-gang portrayal in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62530.Thud___Discworld___34__City_Watch__7_" title="Thud! (Discworld, #34; City Watch #7) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Thud!</a>, Willikins is too dangerous, especially compared to his cherubic characterization in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400354.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch__2_" title="Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47990.Jingo__Discworld___21__City_Watch__4_" title="Jingo (Discworld, #21; City Watch #4) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Jingo</a>. The ending is too happy, wrapped up in a bow. Vimes and Sybil get naked a few times (never happened before, not even when begetting an heir) and the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1885.Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice by Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Pride and Prejudice</a> scenes felt totally out of place, like a peg from a round-world hole. Finally, I found myself a bit grossed out on the repeated focus on snot and poo. But quibbles aside, still a good read!<br><br>Goblins. Regarded as vermin, the cave dwellers are fair game in The Shires. Butcher them, stuff them, snare them, enslave them -- whatever you like. But Commander Vimes has a problem with that...<blockquote>
  <i>"... because once the goblins are vermin, then the poor are vermin, and the dwarfs are vermin, and the trolls are vermin. She wasn't vermin, and she pleaded not to die!"</i>
</blockquote> In the Shires, it's open season on goblins—women and children included. When Vimes stumbles across this hideous but accepted practice he won't stop till he burns the house down. Or burns something. And his wife Sybil won't stop till she changes the world for goblins, one "note" at a time. It's melodic, how Sam and Sybil work in harmony, with the ever-resourceful Willikins adding percussion. As we've seen in the entire series, Pratchett continues to weave a theme of tolerance for all species: <blockquote>
<i>"And Nobby, if you see a goblin who stinks like a latrine and glows slightly blue, well that's a ______ and don't you forget it!" </i><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438397936i/15702377._SY540_.jpg" width="250" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> (Stinky, created by Paul Kidby)</blockquote><br><br><b>What to know more? Setting &amp; General Plot (no spoilers): </b> As the book opens, the commander of the City Watch is forced to take a vacation. Thus, the Duke and Duchess of Ankh (Sam and Sybil) take 6-yr-old Sam to Crundells, otherwise known as Ramkin Hall, the seldom-visited country estate of the Ramkin family — complete with farms, tavern, smithy, trout stream, gallows hill, goblin caves, etc. This is The Shires, located north of Quirm, in the Sto Plains countryside, on the banks of the River Quire ("Old Treachery"). While this bucolic location is appealing to Sybil, it's appalling to Sam. But a nice long break could be good — Sam might finally learn the difference between a robin and a warbler. Certainly, he comes to enjoy spending time navigating along Old Treachery aboard The Wonderful Fanny. He even meets up with Wee Mad Arthur!<br><br>Hoping for something interesting to do in the sleepy Shires, Vimes soon gets his wish. He uncovers a brooding evil (bad bad stuff) and is confronted by a raw police recruit named Constable Feenie Upshot, who takes orders from the local magistrates. Vimes immediately sets out to solve the case (several intertwined crimes, actually) with help from the amazing Willikins and a very young Constable Feenie. He shows Feenie what it really means to be a copper, what the law is, and the nature of human — and nonhuman — rights. The young constable may be wet behind the ears, but he manages to impress and surprise old Vimes. There's plenty of action in the country, to Sam's surprise. He may be getting on in years, but this street fighter doesn't know that! <br><br>Meanwhile, events in Ankh-Morpork move on a roughly parallel course, eventually intersecting with events in the Shires and in Howondaland, a foreign country to the south of Klatch.<br><br>Throughout <i>Snuff,</i> Sam Vimes ponders his intimacy with "The Summoning Dark" (an entity we first met in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/460623708" rel="nofollow noopener">THUD</a>). <br><br>Meanwhile, young Sam ponders the nature of poo. He's six, what else would he do?<br><br><b>Quibbles: </b> The beginning (with Lord Vetinari and Drumknott) didn't have the spirit of Pratchett's previous books, but felt rather like an info dump. The characterization of Willikins does not hold true to his earliest portrayals, including his naïveté in Jingo. Sometimes, I weary of Pratchett's brand of humor. So many puns. Also, sometimes he gets a bit preachy or teachy, especially about justice, social equity, the law, and the price of bread. But I love his way with parody, punnery, and satire, in acceptable amounts.<br><br><b>4 solid stars for <i>Snuff</i></b>. I posted a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/quizzes/results/28423" rel="nofollow noopener">15-question quiz</a> for this book (some clues herein). I listened to the Audible version, narrated nicely by Stephen Briggs. <i>Snuff</i> is the latest (and probably last) addition to the <b>City Watch series,</b> a subset of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/40650-discworld" rel="nofollow noopener">The Discworld Series</a> (City Watch books listed below, in order).<br>_______________________<br><br><b> Series:</b> Here are the Discworld books about the City Watch and its commander Sam Vimes, in order of publication:<br><br>Guards! Guards! (1989) <br>Men at Arms (1993)<br>Feet of Clay (1996)<br>Jingo (1997) <br>The Fifth Elephant (1999) <br>Night Watch (2002) <br>Thud (2005) <br>Snuff (2011) <br>(Series links at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/106221-discworld---ankh-morpork-city-watch">https://www.goodreads.com/series/1062...</a>)<br><br>Also, there are some spin-off books on city modernization featuring con-man turned hero, Moist Von Lipvig, and a few characters from the City Watch. I recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64222.Going_Postal__Discworld___33__Moist_von_Lipwig___1_" title="Going Postal (Discworld, #33; Moist von Lipwig, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Going Postal</a> which describes the invention and proliferation of postage stamps (loved it) and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116296.Making_Money__Discworld___36_" title="Making Money (Discworld, #36) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Making Money</a>, depicting the creation of paper money to replace gold (also very good). Finally, the last book Pratchett wrote for this series is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11275323.Raising_Steam__Discworld___40_" title="Raising Steam (Discworld, #40) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Raising Steam</a>, wherein Moist must make the newly invented locomotive serve Ankh-Morpork and beyond.<br><br> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567670.The_Truth__Discworld___25_" title="The Truth (Discworld, #25) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Truth</a> is not about Moist Von Lipvig, but it has some City Watch characters, including Lord Vetinari, even though it is not officially part of the City Watch series (whatever that means). This book depicts the invention of a printing press and the subsequent introduction of widespread newspapers into society. Satire deals with the wholesale acceptance of lies when ink-validated. William de Worde (he-he) is the city's first investigative journalist, sleuthing out possible murder and a plot to overthrow Lord Vetinari. <br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a> introduces the film industry (Hollywood style) to the eager citizens of Ankh-Morpork and surrounding suburbia. Gaspode the talking dog performs well in this one, with a few City Watch members showing up briefly. This book does not feature Moist Von Lipvig.<br><br><b>I just love the City Watch Series and the Moist spin-offs!</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="7362309288673580598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_227098267" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/227098267" />
    <a title="Heidi Cullinan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2140757-heidi-cullinan"><img alt="Heidi Cullinan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346873524p2/2140757.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/227098267?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heidi Cullinan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heidi Cullinan" href="/user/show/2140757-heidi-cullinan">Heidi Cullinan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2140757-heidi-cullinan?shelf=book-that-influenced-my-writing">book-that-influenced-my-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2140757-heidi-cullinan?shelf=comfort-fun-reads">comfort-fun-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2140757-heidi-cullinan?shelf=absolute-favorites">absolute-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2140757-heidi-cullinan?shelf=plot-character-perfection">plot-character-perfection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2140757-heidi-cullinan?shelf=omfg">omfg</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer227098267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer824688217955164478">Shortly after finishing this book, I took a nap, and as I drifted off I had the thought that if I ever met Sir Terry, I very probably would fall to my knees and start crying and other things that would embarrass us both. The truth is, if we're lucky each of us finds a writer who speaks to our souls and makes our entire being and parts we didn't even know reverberate with something that to describe it as "joy" is a ridiculous understatement. If I were Sir Terry, I would probably describe it as a </span>
  <span id="freeText824688217955164478" style="display:none">Shortly after finishing this book, I took a nap, and as I drifted off I had the thought that if I ever met Sir Terry, I very probably would fall to my knees and start crying and other things that would embarrass us both. The truth is, if we're lucky each of us finds a writer who speaks to our souls and makes our entire being and parts we didn't even know reverberate with something that to describe it as "joy" is a ridiculous understatement. If I were Sir Terry, I would probably describe it as a <i>twang</i> and surround it with descriptors both profound and giggle-inducing at once. Because that, in essence, is Sir Terry Pratchett, may the gods bless his soul.<br /><br />I've said before and will hold <i>Snuff</i> up as further evidence that Pratchett is the Shakespeare of our time, borrowing from here there and everywhere, pinning human nature to the wall and freeing it at once, and never letting a pun go by without a quick flirt and a kiss. Probably this book is best read after having digested the other Vimes books, but maybe not. Thankfully, I'm so versed in Watch books I forget which bit happened in what book because they're just all part of my internal memory now, real people in a real place doing real things. <i>Snuff</i> reminded me of <i>Thud!</i> and not just because of the Summoning Dark; once again Sir Terry took us on a journey of social prejudice while at the same time exploring the never-ending depths of Vimes both as the wonderfulness of Vimes and of good men and humanity in general. I think I prefer <i>Snuff</i> only slightly and because of the Goblins. Leave it to Pratchett to emotionally align you with people who smell like latrines and to once again reveal humans to be both wonderful and hideous at the same time.<br /><br />Perhaps my favorite part of this book, though, was that unlike every book Sir Terry has published since his diagnosis of Alzheimer's, it didn't feel like a goodbye. It was just another Vimes book, as brilliantly done as any other. And by god, I hope any idiot prattering yet again about "I can tell he's sick" gets a visit from Wilikins and his comb. (In double-checking the spelling of that on Google, I see that Rob Wilkins is Sir Terry's real life PA. I did get tears, sap that I am.) Yes, I know that my very favorite writer in the world who has taught me more about writing and story and life than anyone alive or dead, is ill. But like Granny, he aen't dead, and I wish people would quit trying to pluck around him like vultures and diagnose where his disease is getting into his art. Fucking hell -- we are all sick in some way or another, and yes, everything we are and were and shall be influences in our art. That's why they fucking call it art. We take pain and turn it into beauty and stuff that lasts. Tak indeed.<br /><br />Sir Terry, every word you write fills my heart with joy. Selfishly I hope you are able to write many, many more books before Granny holds the door open for you and Death takes you on a walk across the sand. But mostly I hope you are happy and full of the wonder your books give us as much as possible. And I hope you get your wish to meet your end on your terms and not that of your disease.<br /><br />May Anoia keep your drawers sliding freely, and may all your bacon sandwiches be without avec.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="824688217955164478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_227160349" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/227160349" />
    <a title="Lightreads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads"><img alt="Lightreads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230606449p2/1836077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/227160349?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lightreads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lightreads" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads">Lightreads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer227160349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9861497015887449994">Yeah, so I’m always excited to get a new Discworld, and particularly a new Vimes. But…<br/><br/>So it’s about Vimes going on vacation, which for him means foiling a goblin trafficking ring. And I think I like Pratchett better when he’s making fun of systems and institutions – the Death books, <i>Making Money</i> etc. Because sometimes he winds up and he pitches at some great evil, and I have to cover my eyes for a little bit. I had a lot of problems with this one, most of which boil down to how the book wasn’t a</span>
  <span id="freeText9861497015887449994" style="display:none">Yeah, so I’m always excited to get a new Discworld, and particularly a new Vimes. But…<br /><br />So it’s about Vimes going on vacation, which for him means foiling a goblin trafficking ring. And I think I like Pratchett better when he’s making fun of systems and institutions – the Death books, <i>Making Money</i> etc. Because sometimes he winds up and he pitches at some great evil, and I have to cover my eyes for a little bit. I had a lot of problems with this one, most of which boil down to how the book wasn’t actually all that sure that the people being enslaved deserved consideration and compassion. It thought it was sure, and Vimes carried the banner pretty damn well. But the shape of it, the rhetorical flourishes . . . like, just one thing, we had to have a goblin musical prodigy, you see, because if a goblin can play the most complex of <i>human</i> classical music, well then, goblins might actually be people. And the book sort of knew it was playing with the ways we define who gets – let’s call them sentient rights – and who doesn’t. There’s this great scene at the end where Vimes and Vetinari talk at each other about trafficking, one from a natural law stance and one from a positivist stance, and it’s painful and sharp, because Vetinari is saying ‘no, no it was not a crime, and it can’t be until we pass this law making it a crime,’ and Vimes is saying helplessly, ‘no, but, it’s just <i>wrong</i>.’ But there are many many big and small ways that this book was . . . participating in the rhetorical game playing over who goes into what category, and how, and why. It didn't own its own shit, not nearly enough. And it was uncomfortable. <br /><br />Anyway. Still a really good book, and Sam Vimes still basically owns me. And his kid is awesome. But. Uncomfortable.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9861497015887449994" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_225126877" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225126877" />
    <a title="jersey9000" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5239189-jersey9000"><img alt="jersey9000" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365708196p2/5239189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225126877?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jersey9000" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jersey9000" href="/user/show/5239189-jersey9000">jersey9000</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5239189-jersey9000?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi">fantasy-sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225126877" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14947112154990363716"> This book made me sad. Which isn't to say it's a bad book, or a sad book (it is still reasonably funny)- but it made me aware that this is the end of the road for Discworld, or getting very close to it. When he announced some years back that he had begun to suffer from Alzheimer's, I'm sure I wasn't along in dreading the day when the awesome books would stop, when we would have to say goodbye to Discworld. The last book (Unseen Academicals) was not one of the strongest, but still pretty good. T</span>
  <span id="freeText14947112154990363716" style="display:none"> This book made me sad. Which isn't to say it's a bad book, or a sad book (it is still reasonably funny)- but it made me aware that this is the end of the road for Discworld, or getting very close to it. When he announced some years back that he had begun to suffer from Alzheimer's, I'm sure I wasn't along in dreading the day when the awesome books would stop, when we would have to say goodbye to Discworld. The last book (Unseen Academicals) was not one of the strongest, but still pretty good. This one, though, feels like it was written by someone else- the tone is so different from the others. <br /> It feels like someone else wrote this based on his notes and under his supervision. Vimes, the main character, comes off as a parody of himself, and there is far more cursing and profanity in this one than in the previous 38 books (38!) combined, which is why I suspect there was a ghost writer involved in this.<br /> To be clear, this is not a badly written book- far from it, and I think Discworld fans will still enjoy it, even though it's essentially a Sylvester Stallone movie starring Sam Vimes, but it's not the same as the others.<br /> I grew up with these books, started reading them when I was 10, and it's a bummer to realize that a cherished part of your life (and a very influential one, at that- most of my writing is based on doing my best to rip him off, hahaha) could be drawing to a close. He is one of the authors that convinced me to read in the first place, and then later on, to write. Thank you for all you've done, Mr. Pratchett.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14947112154990363716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_251906220" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/251906220" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/767898-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412340362p2/767898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/251906220?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/767898-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/767898-emily?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer251906220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5752596113059786450">I'm a huge fan of the Discworld and the City Watch books in particular but I didn't care for Snuff as much as I could have -- or should have. Down in my gut I feel Commander Sam Vimes has had a great run but now he's so over-powerful, so unbeatable, and full of so many powerful allies (Vetinari, Lady Sybil, his unstoppable assassin-butler, the demon who lives in his head, every City Watch post ever, etc) he's no longer much of a joy to read. He has no challenge. He has no mountain to climb. The </span>
  <span id="freeText5752596113059786450" style="display:none">I'm a huge fan of the Discworld and the City Watch books in particular but I didn't care for Snuff as much as I could have -- or should have. Down in my gut I feel Commander Sam Vimes has had a great run but now he's so over-powerful, so unbeatable, and full of so many powerful allies (Vetinari, Lady Sybil, his unstoppable assassin-butler, the demon who lives in his head, every City Watch post ever, etc) he's no longer much of a joy to read. He has no challenge. He has no mountain to climb. The term for this is Mary Sue, and Vimes has become a Mary Sue character. <br /><br />I would have happily rolled with Vimes, Duke of Ankh-Morpork if the book had turned into a commentary on Upstairs-Downstairs like it promised in the beginning, or would have kept to the city and focused on the goblins, or simply had more focus /in general/. Too much was going on and not enough was going on that had focus. We had some Class Warfare AND smuggling AND murderers AND drugs AND poor oppressed goblins whom no one understands AND What Happens to Fred Colon AND Vimes Taking Charge... the book lacked focus and the lack of focus took away from the more interesting action sequences and themes. Oppression bad, yes. But it didn't have the feeling of freeing an oppressed people like, say, Feet of Clay did, even though it was, at its core, the same story.<br /><br /> I would have been happier, perhaps with two books: Vimes investigating a MURDER in a Countryside Upstairs-Downstairs and a more focused story about the Goblins. Or something to that effect. Much like Unseen Academicals, Snuff is a long way from being unreadable but I had to force myself to finish it. It didn't grab me the same way Discworld books normally do. It's no "The Times" or "Going Postal." If I had to rank them, Snuff would dwell somewhere in the bottom third. <br /><br />A high point: the continuation of Wee Mad Arthur's education as a Nac Mac Feegle from _I Shall Wear Midnight_. I adore the Feegles and having one who isn't Rob Anybody's crew is always good. <br /><br />Here's hoping PTerry still has a few books left in him -- and if they are City Watch books, they star Carrot and Angua and Cheery and the crew. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5752596113059786450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review251906220">
  <td colspan="2">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194308870?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194308870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer956810049925556987">It looks like Ankh-Morpork is going to have to do without City Watch Commander Sir Samuel Vimes for a couple of weeks. Lady Sybil has decided that Young Sam, now an inquisitive six-year-old, needs some fresh country air and healthy outdoor recreation at Ramkin Hall, the family's country estate. It's the last thing that Vimes wants: trees and daffodils and chirping birds and his wife supervising his every meal for two whole weeks. No bacon sandwiches? Some vacation. <br/><br/>But Lady Sybil has a way of ge</span>
  <span id="freeText956810049925556987" style="display:none">It looks like Ankh-Morpork is going to have to do without City Watch Commander Sir Samuel Vimes for a couple of weeks. Lady Sybil has decided that Young Sam, now an inquisitive six-year-old, needs some fresh country air and healthy outdoor recreation at Ramkin Hall, the family's country estate. It's the last thing that Vimes wants: trees and daffodils and chirping birds and his wife supervising his every meal for two whole weeks. No bacon sandwiches? Some vacation. <br /><br />But Lady Sybil has a way of getting her way, which is how Vimes finds himself rambling around his estate and stopping in at the Goblin's Head for a non-alcoholic pint. Goblin's Head - the quaint country pub with little windows of wavery glass and...is that an actual goblin's head mounted above the bar? <br /><br />Vimes is still not comfortable with his status as the estate owner, and it is obvious that the local blacksmith isn't either. Mutterings and meaningful glares start his copper senses tingling and he realizes there's much more going on than a little working-class discontent. By the next morning the blacksmith is missing, the hillside where Vimes agreed to meet him at midnight is covered in blood, and Vimes finds himself under suspicion of murder. <br /><br />In my opinion Sir Terry Pratchett can do no wrong, and I know that his fans the world over will delight in this 39th novel in the Discworld saga. Wry humor, quirky unforgettable characters, and a plot that hurls itself down the Old Treachery River with the speed of a two-cow barge. (Okay, so maybe faster than that.) Readers will come away even more in love with the dogged determination and conflicted soul of the City Watch's most dedicated policeman. <br /><br />Disclaimer: Advanced Reader eGalley from the publisher <br /><br />keywords: Discworld, goblins, crime, comedy</span>
  <a data-text-id="956810049925556987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FBfRXScij1gkv76loovDRJI0ltfrOMijOJEX14/Hc8SbSFi7q5nPNRn32kOWlHLKl6TlVZJEc0p/vxHpw8fOYw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YARau1z+NKI1E+3BNfnfUGk5TBO0nhrU32cv6koBMsnvW9Nd0EV0zwhbiScB5m7ebKk/kc3ioT2YSSnUBgGPbg==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8978304309576525865">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br/><br/>Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, i</span>
  <span id="freeText8978304309576525865" style="display:none">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel, <i>The Color of Magic</i>, in 1983. In 1987, he turned to writing full time. <br /><br />There are over 40 books in the Discworld series, of which four are written for children. The first of these, <i>The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents</i>, won the Carnegie Medal. <br /><br />A non-Discworld book, <i>Good Omens</i>, his 1990 collaboration with Neil Gaiman, has been a longtime bestseller and was reissued in hardcover by William Morrow in early 2006 (it is also available as a mass market paperback - Harper Torch, 2006 - and trade paperback - Harper Paperbacks, 2006). <br /><br />In 2008, Harper Children's published Terry's standalone non-Discworld YA novel, <i>Nation</i>. Terry published <i>Snuff</i> in October 2011. <br /><br />Regarded as one of the most significant contemporary English-language satirists, Pratchett has won numerous literary awards, was named an Officer of the British Empire (OBE) “for services to literature” in 1998, and has received honorary doctorates from the University of Warwick in 1999, the University of Portsmouth in 2001, the University of Bath in 2003, the University of Bristol in 2004, Buckinghamshire New University in 2008, the University of Dublin in 2008, Bradford University in 2009, the University of Winchester in 2009, and The Open University in 2013 for his contribution to Public Service.<br /><br />In Dec. of 2007, Pratchett disclosed that he had been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. On 18 Feb, 2009, he was knighted by Queen Elizabeth II.<br /><br />He was awarded the World Fantasy Life Achievement Award in 2010.<br /><br />Sir Terry Pratchett passed away on 12th March 2015.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8978304309576525865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
